    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8178796948115075433"><i>The Misanthrope</i> or <i>Le Misanthrope ou l’Atrabilaire amoureux</i> is a comedy of manners in five acts and in verse.<br/><br/>It is one of the best of Molière's plays — and one of the greatest of all comedies — spotlighting the absurdities of social and literary pretension, focusing on a man who is quick to criticize the faults of others, yet remains blind to his own.<br/>This play satirizes th</span>
  <span id="freeText8178796948115075433" style="display:none"><i>The Misanthrope</i> or <i>Le Misanthrope ou l’Atrabilaire amoureux</i> is a comedy of manners in five acts and in verse.<br /><br />It is one of the best of Molière's plays — and one of the greatest of all comedies — spotlighting the absurdities of social and literary pretension, focusing on a man who is quick to criticize the faults of others, yet remains blind to his own.<br />This play satirizes the hypocrisies of French aristocratic society, but it also engages a more serious tone when pointing out the flaws, which all humans possess. The play differs from other farces at the time by employing dynamic characters like Alceste and Célimène as opposed to the traditionally flat characters used by most satirists to criticize problems in society. It also differs from most of Molière's other works by focusing more on character development and nuances than on plot progression. The play, though not a commercial success in its time, survives as Molière's best-known work today. Much of its universal appeal is due to common undercurrents of misanthropy across cultural borders.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8178796948115075433" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

    <div id=buyButtonContainer class="uitext buttons buyableFeature u-marginTopXSmall u-paddingBottomXSmall u-bottomGrayBorder" data-book-id=752994 >

    <div class="u-positionRelative">

<div id="review_715071268" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/715071268" />
    <a title="Florencia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia"><img alt="Florencia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601866797p2/21575078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/715071268?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Florencia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Florencia" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia">Florencia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=french">french</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer715071268" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4506859909720763000">This play was written in the 17th century and it is so contemporary, because of that feeling of rejection towards society and almost everything that surrounds it. And... the subsequent isolation that the person feels, of course. <br/><br/>It happens all the time. People who are sick of other people but then, they can't stand that deep loneliness they find. They were never ready for it, and they end up believing they are indeed cursed or something and kind of give up, accepting that miserable existence; ve</span>
  <span id="freeText4506859909720763000" style="display:none">This play was written in the 17th century and it is so contemporary, because of that feeling of rejection towards society and almost everything that surrounds it. And... the subsequent isolation that the person feels, of course. <br /><br />It happens all the time. People who are sick of other people but then, they can't stand that deep loneliness they find. They were never ready for it, and they end up believing they are indeed cursed or something and kind of give up, accepting that miserable existence; very Harry Haller. They might accept it until they find that special someone that dislikes that side of the world, too. That "What! You too? I thought that no one but myself..." moment. Or they die alone, whatever comes first. <br /><br />So, 17th century, today. No matter the time or place, some people feel like outsiders, and that brings, inevitably, alienation. That "other people" start to look at them like the personification of abnormality. Like human beings that just hate humanity for the hell of it. Like misanthropes. And that is the word that led me to this book. I wanted to read this one because I used to think I was one of them. <br /><br />Back to the book. It is an insightful play about the essence of human nature, our virtues and flaws. Molière brilliantly portrayed the hypocrisy of its time through Alceste, the protagonist. A man with a profound existential conflict that fell in love with Célimène, a 17th-century French Holly Golightly. Oh, yes, that can't go wrong, right?<br /><br />I really enjoyed reading this book. Funny stuff with actually some meaning.<br /><br /><br />Oct 20, 14?<br />* Also on <a href="https://nonbookreviews.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">my blog</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4506859909720763000" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2927888191229542561">Why has it taken me so long to read this? What entertainment! Hilarious, dramatic entertainment! I'm sorry I'm gushing a little too much, but <b>I was not expecting this to be so funny and good.</b> After watching <a href='http://www.imdb.com/title/tt2207050/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Alceste à Bicyclette</a> (thank you again to a classmate of mine from French for the recommendation), I planned on reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/752994.The_Misanthrope' title='The Misanthrope by Molière' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Misanthrope</a> and Molière's other works.   <span id="freeText2927888191229542561" style="display:none">Why has it taken me so long to read this? What entertainment! Hilarious, dramatic entertainment! I'm sorry I'm gushing a little too much, but <b>I was not expecting this to be so funny and good.</b> After watching <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt2207050/" rel="nofollow noopener">Alceste à Bicyclette</a> (thank you again to a classmate of mine from French for the recommendation), I planned on reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/752994.The_Misanthrope" title="The Misanthrope by Molière" rel="nofollow noopener">The Misanthrope</a> and Molière's other works. I'm delighted that I did. Every time I thought things were going to calm down with its dramatics, it kept escalating more, and more, and more. <b>Every Scene, every Act, every word that came out of Alceste's mouth (and a few other characters') was amusing.</b> Perhaps I'm not taking this in like others or reading this Play like how it's supposed to be read, but frankly, my dear, I don't give a damn. <br><br>I really want to see this play in action--on Broadway . . . and done well.<br><br>I definitely see myself reading this over and over again. Act's III and V are my favourites. <br><br>~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~<br><br><b>Pre-review:</b><br><br>Literally my expressions (in order) after finishing this.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1408087452ra/10805512.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1408087452ra/10805513.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1408087452ra/10805514.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Short rambling to come... when my cheeks stop hurting from laughing and smiling with amusement too much.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2882387566?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/3143117-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 0245522204" href="/book/show/25154940-the-misanthrope">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3143117-david?shelf=french-lit">french-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3143117-david?shelf=francais">francais</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2882387566" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3818912915261609462">A name can be a funny thing. For years I couldn’t bring myself to read something about a person who hated society, people or culture. Sure I had heard that Molière was a great French writer, but what did I know about his masterpiece? Ríen. <br/><br/>Recently I just happened to see a favourable review by a Portuguese friend and I decided to take a chance. Out of the chaos of a masked and “fake” society comes a pursuit of truth, peace and harmony. Ah this is leading somewhere.<br/><br/>If a society was so tainted by </span>
  <span id="freeText3818912915261609462" style="display:none">A name can be a funny thing. For years I couldn’t bring myself to read something about a person who hated society, people or culture. Sure I had heard that Molière was a great French writer, but what did I know about his masterpiece? Ríen. <br /><br />Recently I just happened to see a favourable review by a Portuguese friend and I decided to take a chance. Out of the chaos of a masked and “fake” society comes a pursuit of truth, peace and harmony. Ah this is leading somewhere.<br /><br />If a society was so tainted by corruption, social climbing and a pursuit of happiness by any means, one could, well, become disallusioned. Perhaps even a misanthrope. Throw in a love for a frivolous woman, set oneself against one of her lovers and work oneself through the in’s and out’s of language, and you would be fit to be tied. Toss in 12 syllable Alexandrian verse and you are in for a pleasant, humorous romp in the fashions of 17th century France.<br /><br />I was taught Shakespeare in high school. I wish we had been given Molière as well. Such grand fun!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3818912915261609462" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText15159738670045222968" style="display:none">That was a pleasantly entertaining read!<br>Although I think I would have enjoyed it more if I had more time to really read and comprehend instead of plowing through each act in less than two days plus a billion other assignments!<br><br><i>Dear French professor,<br><br>Thank you.<br><br>Sincerely,<br>Sincerely Ungrateful</i><br><br>So I think the silliest part was the wacky love polygon, or as I mentioned in a status update, <b>love mashed potatoes</b>. <br>I used Microsoft Word to create a diagram that shows you all the people and the target(s) of their affection at all points in the story mushed together. Also, as you can see, Word decided I spelled everything wrong. I did not, I promise. These are the correct spellings.<br>Note: I would only waste my time doing this for you guys. I hope you appreciate my manual labor!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1475805097i/20768031.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In short, I would recommend! It's philosophical and fun, so why not?</span>
  <a data-text-id="15159738670045222968" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_614661115" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/614661115" />
    <a title="Linda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3849889-linda"><img alt="Linda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307400163p2/3849889.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/614661115?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Linda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Linda" href="/user/show/3849889-linda">Linda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3849889-linda?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3849889-linda?shelf=french-literature">french-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3849889-linda?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3849889-linda?shelf=plays">plays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer614661115" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5842490320491702275">Molière, or Jean-Baptiste Poquelin, satirizes french 17th century social hypocrisy. The main character despises all forms of false, insincere remarks and thinks people’s pride should be about honesty and not good behavior and proper welcome. The comical situations that arises are a consequence of such an opinion. He gets into trouble for being truthfully honest, which border on rudeness and earn him enemies. <br/><br/><br/>Spoilers!<br/><br/>The funniest thing is that the woman who might be the worst of them all, who lo</span>
  <span id="freeText5842490320491702275" style="display:none">Molière, or Jean-Baptiste Poquelin, satirizes french 17th century social hypocrisy. The main character despises all forms of false, insincere remarks and thinks people’s pride should be about honesty and not good behavior and proper welcome. The comical situations that arises are a consequence of such an opinion. He gets into trouble for being truthfully honest, which border on rudeness and earn him enemies. <br /><br /><br />Spoilers!<br /><br />The funniest thing is that the woman who might be the worst of them all, who loves gossip and talking behind people’s backs, is the one he is smitten with, and of course he hates her behavior as much as he hates his own feelings.<br /><br />End of spoilers!<br /><br /><br />Moliere’s writing is darkly humoristic and delightful. <br /><br />About esteem:<br />"There's precious little satisfaction in the most glorious of reputations if you find you have to share it with the whole universe. Esteem must be founded on some sort of preference. Bestow it on everybody and it ceases to have any meaning at all." - Alceste<br /><br />About friendship:<br />"Indeed it is a profanation of the word to use it on every occasion." - Alceste<br /><br />About authors:<br />"The only people who can be excused for unleashing a bad book on the world are the poor devils who have to write for a living." - Alceste<br /><br />About flattery:<br />"Flatterers are always to blame for the vices which prevail among mankind." - Alceste<br /><br />"The more you love someone, the less you should flatter them. The proof of true love is to be unsparing in fault-finding." - Alceste<br /><br />About men justifying their own love choices:<br />"He counts her defects as perfections or finds flattering names for them. If she's pale, it's the pale beauty of the jasmine flower. She may be swarthy enough to frighten the horses, but for him she's an adorable brunette. If she's thin, she's slender and graceful; if fat, she has a queenly dignity; if she neglects her appearance, slight though her attractions may be, she is said to have a 'careless beauty; if she's tall, she'll have the majesty of a goddess; if she's short, she's an abridged version of all the virtues under heaven! If she's proud, her nature is regal. If she's sly, she's clever. If she's stupid, she's all heart. If she talks all the time, she's cheerful. If she never talks at all, she's proper and modest. And so it is that the true and passionate lover worships the very faults of the woman he loves." - Éliante<br /><br />About love and suffering:<br />"When we suffer at the hands of the person we love, we make many a plan that we never carry out." - Éliante<br /><br />About the purpose of virtue:<br />"If all men were righteous, all hearts true and frank and loyal, what purpose would most of our virtues serve? Their usefulness lies in enabling us to stay calm and bear the injustices others inflict upon us when we are in the right." - Philinte<br /><br />The only shortcoming, which unfortunately is rather prominent, is the somewhat weak ending. Anyway, the play was interesting and the dialogue was acute. I started to wonder how the world would be like if everyone was like Alceste. What kind of society it would produce. At first I thought what a wonderful thing: No lies and no misconseptions. But then - with a flicker of irony - I came to the realization: What a horrible experience! People are not ready! They have too much evil and hurtful thoughts for them to be let loose without consideration. They would kill each other in pure anger and contempt. But it’s a beautiful thought, the thought of honesty.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5842490320491702275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_366526578" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/366526578" />
    <a title="David Sarkies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies"><img alt="David Sarkies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470027282p2/5856247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/366526578?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sarkies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sarkies" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies">David Sarkies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Lovers of French comedy</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to David by: </span>
      A bookshop
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5856247-david-sarkies?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer366526578" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4612681018807538331"><b>The philosopher and the aristocratic woman</b><br/>25 March 2013<br/><br/>	One of the things that I like about reading plays is that they tend to be short and are easy to get through in a short amount of time. However the problem I have is that since they tend to be written so as to be performed on stage (in the same way that poetry is generally written to be read aloud) it makes it difficult to actually follow what is happening. With regards to many of Shakespeare's plays this is not a problem because you can fin</span>
  <span id="freeText4612681018807538331" style="display:none"><b>The philosopher and the aristocratic woman</b><br />25 March 2013<br /><br />	One of the things that I like about reading plays is that they tend to be short and are easy to get through in a short amount of time. However the problem I have is that since they tend to be written so as to be performed on stage (in the same way that poetry is generally written to be read aloud) it makes it difficult to actually follow what is happening. With regards to many of Shakespeare's plays this is not a problem because you can find most of them on You Tube, however a quick search through Youtube has not provided me with any joy in regards to this particular play.<br />	The Misanthrope was the only play of Moliere's that I had heard of until a friend of mine bought me a collection of his plays for Christmas. The reason that I knew of it was because I would see the Dover Thrift edition sitting on bookshop shelves and I did want to get around to reading it some day (particularly since it was short). I guess I have done that now.<br />	The Misanthrope is about a poet who is disgusted with the fake attitudes of the French nobility, however is in love with a woman who is the epitome of this attitude. I guess I can relate to this because I have been infatuated with women like this myself, and there is that part of me that believes that I might be able to change her. Mind you, this love is not unrequited simply because she also has feelings for Alceste (the misanthrope of the title) but is torn between her love for him, and for living the high life at court – that is being a flirt.<br />	As mentioned, I can relate to Alceste a lot, particularly since I can be very critical of the shallowness of the western lifestyle, but have also been in love with women that have epitomised this lifestyle. Once again the love was not necessarily unrequited, however the difference was that these women, while appreciating being wined and dined, in the end only really wanted one thing (a free meal), where as I preferred good conversation and a companion. I guess I am just a bit conservative in that fashion.<br />	It is interesting to see how things have not necessarily changed since the days of Moliere, though I would suggest that more people are able to live this lifestyle now than they were in those days. In fact on my recent visit to China (okay, it was only across the border to Shenzhen, and it was only for a day, but it did give me a bit of a taste) I could see how many of the people there were dressed like Westerners and wondered around the streets of Shenzhen with their faces glued to their smartphones (and I suspect that they were generally playing games). Personally I cannot comment on the Chinese culture, particularly on the Mainland, but there does appear to be little different from our culture.<br />	This is the key I suspect because what Alceste is criticising is the shallowness of the culture. He is a poet, and obviously a thinker as well, so he (like me) likes to analyse things and to try to understand why things are the way they are, while the people around him care only for the pleasure and luxury of the high life. However Alceste is still human, he is attracted to a beautiful woman, and she is nothing like the person who he is: she is shallow and only interested in flirting and living the high life. I guess that is why Alceste walks out on her and on his society at the end, simply because he knows that he is not going to change her, and that if he hates this society so much, he might as well leave.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4612681018807538331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I know that this means the author did an amazing job capturing the hypocrisy of the characters. However, maybe he did too good a job if the characters of Alceste and Celimene are able to make me so angry it spills over toward the work. I think the character of Alceste reminds me of how I acted when I was in high school and that I was "cool" to be sarcastic and find fault with movies or books, rather th</span>
  <span id="freeText12368931760488836379" style="display:none">I have a hard time rating books or plays when I want to throttle the characters near to death. I know that this means the author did an amazing job capturing the hypocrisy of the characters. However, maybe he did too good a job if the characters of Alceste and Celimene are able to make me so angry it spills over toward the work. I think the character of Alceste reminds me of how I acted when I was in high school and that I was "cool" to be sarcastic and find fault with movies or books, rather than to admit liking things and talking about the good things. I am glad I am not that person anymore and doubly glad I didn't have to move to a desert island away from humanity to achieve it. Kudos to Moliere, a pox to Alceste.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_559557780" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/559557780" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8391461-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422737926p2/8391461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/559557780?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/8391461-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer559557780" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14023735662807463869">I tend to side more with critics who deem this play a comedy rather than a tragicomedy. Molière refused to conform with the traditional comedic structure of his time, and ended his play by ending the relationship of the central couple... and I'm thankful for that. Alceste's exhausting, relentless railing of mankind's hypocrisy and feigned sincerity is juxtaposed with Célimène's desperate attachment to a society of individuals she finds repulsive - where knowing the right person is important only</span>
  <span id="freeText14023735662807463869" style="display:none">I tend to side more with critics who deem this play a comedy rather than a tragicomedy. Molière refused to conform with the traditional comedic structure of his time, and ended his play by ending the relationship of the central couple... and I'm thankful for that. Alceste's exhausting, relentless railing of mankind's hypocrisy and feigned sincerity is juxtaposed with Célimène's desperate attachment to a society of individuals she finds repulsive - where knowing the right person is important only to create reputation - to illustrate that total honesty and social compromise do not easily co-exist. These characters were not meant to end up together; their love for one another is meant to be poisonous and, in Célimène's case, artificial. The reconciliation of these two characters would have completely altered Molière's intended message, so I strongly disagree with readers who wish for a different ending.<br /><br />The occasional zingers spread throughout the play helped to tame my rising irritation for the characters. The rising action became dull and monotonous at times, but I was satisfied again by the end as Alceste was shrugged off by the other characters, forcing him to act on his big talk of running away from pathetic humanity. Definitely more comedic than I was expecting, but you have to remember this was written in 1666, people.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14023735662807463869" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2535458031" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2535458031" />
    <a title="Inkspill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/79575646-inkspill"><img alt="Inkspill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599024311p2/79575646.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2535458031?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inkspill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inkspill" href="/user/show/79575646-inkspill">Inkspill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/79575646-inkspill?shelf=drama-script-4-stage-screen-audio">drama-script-4-stage-screen-audio</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2535458031" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9132198365707000025">A few months back I read a modern rendition of this play by Ranjit Bolt, <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2477321530'>https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> with no knowledge of this play is about but it made me aware of this play.<br/><br/>The story. Alceste loathes modern society’s double standards, and is equally irritated by his good friend, Philinte, who is no different. Unphased, Philinte argues that it’s kinder not to be blunt and say what you really think of someone. This banter runs through the play whilst Alceste also prods his girlfrien</span>
  <span id="freeText9132198365707000025" style="display:none">A few months back I read a modern rendition of this play by Ranjit Bolt, <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2477321530">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> with no knowledge of this play is about but it made me aware of this play.<br /><br />The story. Alceste loathes modern society’s double standards, and is equally irritated by his good friend, Philinte, who is no different. Unphased, Philinte argues that it’s kinder not to be blunt and say what you really think of someone. This banter runs through the play whilst Alceste also prods his girlfriend, Celimene, to show more signs of fidelity. She’s young, attractive and a flatterer who attracts many admirers. His need for honesty and frankness is admired, well at a (real huge distance) distance, as he discovers that the legal system is also not free from double standards.<br /><br />This is a story Moliere delivers as a comedy in poetical form. This made it a fun read, especially when the subtext was dealing with a topic that is just as current today. But Moliere does not favour either side, instead he lets his characters debate and defend their point of view, and he does with comedy.<br /><br />This year, slowly I am discovering that how a work is translated has a bearing on the reading experience. I read 3 versions of Moliere’s Misanthrope, including the one that is available for free but my favourite was included in <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52822.The_Misanthrope_Tartuffe_and_Other_Play">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5...</a>, here, and I say this not being able to read Moliere’s work in French, so I am speaking from purely a dramatic pov, how the characters clashing in social values is paced and delivered in poetry made this a very entertaining read for me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9132198365707000025" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3393301447" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2831559382461851395">I feel like Molière would be a lot of fun in the social media era. Alceste refuses to play nice in the societal sandbox, as he refuses to soften his harsh opinions in a world that seems much more suited to flattery and phoniness. There is something weirdly entertaining about combining mocking subject matter with sing-songy rhymed couplets… but still, I would like to give this a try in the original French someday!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2761864503" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2761864503" />
    <a title="Tamar Nagel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42237904-tamar-nagel"><img alt="Tamar Nagel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582580871p2/42237904.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2761864503?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tamar Nagel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tamar Nagel" href="/user/show/42237904-tamar-nagel">Tamar Nagel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42237904-tamar-nagel?shelf=plays">plays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2761864503" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10126434489492512912">This play is hilarious. I was smiling from start to finish! I was also, however, a bit troubled at how deeply I identified with Alceste. His remarks to Philinte like, “Leave me alone; you spoil my solitude,” were brushed aside in such a loving, careless way. I heartily enjoyed their dynamic. <br/><br/>Alceste’s bluntness and utter lack of hypocrisy or equivocation were so humorously described. His firm principled nature that does not compromise, much to his own detriment, really resonated with me because </span>
  <span id="freeText10126434489492512912" style="display:none">This play is hilarious. I was smiling from start to finish! I was also, however, a bit troubled at how deeply I identified with Alceste. His remarks to Philinte like, “Leave me alone; you spoil my solitude,” were brushed aside in such a loving, careless way. I heartily enjoyed their dynamic. <br /><br />Alceste’s bluntness and utter lack of hypocrisy or equivocation were so humorously described. His firm principled nature that does not compromise, much to his own detriment, really resonated with me because I often do the exact same thing. Moliere was able to capture so much with just a few brushstrokes. <br /><br />Excellent drama, excellent characters, and a healthy dose of pithy quotes to remember. This is a comedy that contains a lot of wisdom.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10126434489492512912" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_872634476" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/872634476" />
    <a title="Ben" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9592279-ben"><img alt="Ben" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1533140387p2/9592279.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/872634476?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben" href="/user/show/9592279-ben">Ben</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer872634476" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5501140794273830649"><i>The Misanthrope</i> it is said is one of Molière's best known works, and as this is the fourth Molière play that I've read, I find that a shame, as I found <i>Tartuffe</i> and <i>The School for Wives</i> both superior to this work. Unfortunately, best known does not always mean the best developed or the smartest; we could point just as well here to the well-knownness of Marx's <i>The Communist Manifesto</i> as well, his most famous but perhaps least developed work. These two examples are both excellent works in their ow</span>
  <span id="freeText5501140794273830649" style="display:none"><i>The Misanthrope</i> it is said is one of Molière's best known works, and as this is the fourth Molière play that I've read, I find that a shame, as I found <i>Tartuffe</i> and <i>The School for Wives</i> both superior to this work. Unfortunately, best known does not always mean the best developed or the smartest; we could point just as well here to the well-knownness of Marx's <i>The Communist Manifesto</i> as well, his most famous but perhaps least developed work. These two examples are both excellent works in their own rights, but are not representative of the quality of the whole body of work of their respective authors. Less plot-driven than his other plays, <i>The Misanthrope</i> is more concerned with ideas and character development, and like the best authors, Molière really leaves it up to the audience to determine whether or not his title character is merited in his philosophic views or not. The play explores issues of brutal sincerity versus hypocrisy in social affairs, something I feel is better communicated in <i>Tartuffe,</i> though not in exactly the same manner. And the misanthropic title character in Shakespeare's <i>Timon of Athens</i> is, in my opinion, better developed, and the ideas driving his misanthropy are more thoroughly and critically explored.<br /><br />That said, while this comedy of errors play was not my favorite by Molière and while I feel there are better plays that explore the themes with which the playwright was here concerned, it is not without significant merits. The characters are well-developed. The ideas explored are very interesting. And Molière presents his characters in this work more objectively than he does in other plays, as mentioned briefly above. In <i>The School for Wives</i> Arnolphe is obviously a fool. In <i>Tartuffe</i> the title character is a hypocrite and Orgon and his mother are evidently blinded by Tartuffe's proclaimed virtue. In this play, Célimène is an obvious hypocrite, but Alceste (the misanthrope) is honest to a fault, viewing the social customs of the day and social niceties as detestable features of modern man, and we are not necessarily (just by the text alone) positioned to identify with him or to oppose him (though to my understanding, actors often portray him in a way that sways the audience to align against him). But as readers I do not feel that we are necessarily positioned to be either for or against Alceste. It is easy to see his faults, a man who seems to adhere to a Hobbesian view of human nature, but his views are not necessarily unwarranted. And, if the ideas/themes are explored better elsewhere and if other works have stronger plot elements, the main character's depth is perhaps what does make this play such a memorable work of the playwright's. It may be a good introductory work to Molière, but if readers are not won over by it, I would caution that it is not entirely representative of his style. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5501140794273830649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_832261896" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/832261896" />
    <a title="Norrin2" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/888463-norrin2"><img alt="Norrin2" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1380469873p2/888463.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/832261896?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Norrin2" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Norrin2" href="/user/show/888463-norrin2">Norrin2</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer832261896" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10763531741277077346">My first Moliere and what I wanted to know was can a play written by a Frenchman in 1666 amuse an American 350 years later? In other words is this comedy written in such a different place and time it might as well be another planet still funny?<br/>I'm happy to say the answer is "Mais oui!" This book made me laugh out loud several times. I loved Alceste's bitter but witty rants about how horrible everybody he knows is. His review of a rival's sonnet is so scathing he lands in court. <br/>"But gentlemen, I</span>
  <span id="freeText10763531741277077346" style="display:none">My first Moliere and what I wanted to know was can a play written by a Frenchman in 1666 amuse an American 350 years later? In other words is this comedy written in such a different place and time it might as well be another planet still funny?<br />I'm happy to say the answer is "Mais oui!" This book made me laugh out loud several times. I loved Alceste's bitter but witty rants about how horrible everybody he knows is. His review of a rival's sonnet is so scathing he lands in court. <br />"But gentlemen, I cannot praise his rhyme.<br />In fact it ought to be a capital crime<br />For anyone so sadly unendowed<br />To write a sonnet and read the thing aloud."<br />I was genuinely interested in which of Celinene's many wooers would win her and was pleased and surprised at how the play ended. A great deal of the credit for my enjoyment of Moliere has to do with the great translation by Richard Wilbur, himself a Pulitzer Prize winning poet. </span>

Good sense avoids all extremes, and requires us to be soberly rational. . . I, as well as yourself, notice a hundred things every day which might be better managed, differently enacted; but whatever I may discover at any moment, people do not see me in a rage like you. <div id="review_1181630989" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1181630989" />
    <a title="Emilie Andersen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15451467-emilie-andersen"><img alt="Emilie Andersen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517076423p2/15451467.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<div id="review_3431903062" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3431903062" />
    <a title="Yashaswini" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53591372-yashaswini"><img alt="Yashaswini" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3431903062?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Yashaswini" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yashaswini" href="/user/show/53591372-yashaswini">Yashaswini</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3431903062" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer216177857368494623">If someone had given me a copy of the first act of Molière’s “The Misanthrope” without mentioning the author or the period in which it was written, I'd have assumed this to be a piece of well-written contemporary literature. The misanthrope is a timeless classic known for its bold and refreshing take on the social pretensions of the 17th-century French aristocracy. I knew I’d done the right thing by picking this book up when I read the following conversation between the tactful Philinte and the </span>
  <span id="freeText216177857368494623" style="display:none">If someone had given me a copy of the first act of Molière’s “The Misanthrope” without mentioning the author or the period in which it was written, I'd have assumed this to be a piece of well-written contemporary literature. The misanthrope is a timeless classic known for its bold and refreshing take on the social pretensions of the 17th-century French aristocracy. I knew I’d done the right thing by picking this book up when I read the following conversation between the tactful Philinte and the cynical Alceste, who also happens to be the play’s protagonist.<br /><br /><i>Philinte: But when a stranger rushes up and hugs you tight,<br />You have to hug him back––it seems only polite;<br />You can’t keep him at arm’s length, you must play the game,<br />And if he swears you’re his best friend, you do the same.<br /><br />Alceste: No. Your hypocrisy disgusts me, and that’s flat.<br />You fashionable types, you all behave like that.<br />I tell you, I can’t stand the phoney posturing<br />Of men who claim they like you more than anything,<br />And fling their arms around you, kiss you on the cheek,<br />Insist that they adore you, every time they speak.<br />They’ve perfect manners, and they all obey the rules,<br />But decent men are treated on a par with fools.<br />Look, I mean, what’s the point of someone kissing you,<br />And swearing he appreciates and loves you too,<br />Proclaiming to the world that you’re the only one,<br />Then rushing off to do the same to everyone?<br />No. If a man who has the slightest self-respect<br />Is faced with such hypocrisy, he must object.<br />For very little’s added to our sense of worth<br />If we’re the same as all the others on this earth.<br />If you respect a man, believing he’s the best,<br />Don’t put him on an equal footing with the rest.” </i><br /><br />What blew me away was how nothing much seems to have changed in all these years. Alceste’s scathing remarks on "fashionable" people with "phoney posturing," who <i>"claim to like you more than anything, and fling their arms around you, kiss you on the cheek, and insist that they adore you, every time they speak…” </i> are accurate, and surprisingly relevant in today’s day and age. I know many people who conduct themselves similarly and consider themselves a class apart for networking so guilefully. The only problem I have with people who don’t shy away from flattering others generously is that somewhere down the line, genuine appreciation gets confused for politeness. <br />Alceste wreaks havoc with his brutally honest opinions and isn't slightly remorseful of its consequences. He makes his vehement and intense disregard for flattery, insincerity, falsehoods, and pointless social facade, evident with his razor-sharp criticisms and witticisms when the occasion calls for it. The aristocratic society’s disdain for and mockery of Alceste doesn’t leave him unfazed or embarrassed. Funnily enough, the satire is centred around Alceste’s love for a woman who values precisely what he despises.<br />I do wish the play was longer and had delved into the crux of Alceste’s misanthropy. Although this entertaining play raises several crucial points and thought-provoking ideas, I wouldn’t want to go with the alternatives Molière presents.</span>
  <a data-text-id="216177857368494623" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1034376222429820683">Another one I put off for a while and for all the wrong reasons. I thought it would be blatantly unfunny and daunting to read. <br/><br/>I really felt for Alceste. He despises society and wants more than anything to escape it, which is totally paradoxical since he is desperately in love with this total cow of a woman who manipulates and lies to Alceste and others. On top of that, he is rarely seen without his close and surprisingly optimistic friend Philinte ( I mean come on, 10 seconds with Alceste and y</span>
  <span id="freeText1034376222429820683" style="display:none">Another one I put off for a while and for all the wrong reasons. I thought it would be blatantly unfunny and daunting to read. <br /><br />I really felt for Alceste. He despises society and wants more than anything to escape it, which is totally paradoxical since he is desperately in love with this total cow of a woman who manipulates and lies to Alceste and others. On top of that, he is rarely seen without his close and surprisingly optimistic friend Philinte ( I mean come on, 10 seconds with Alceste and you would be miserable as hell even if the man is speaking total truth ) <br /><br />Alceste brings to light the flaws of society, making the importance of honesty very clear to us. There is definitely a lot to ponder upon here. </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('i1QG1ECYBAOBbFX5/YibE0z8U13fWNnuIGXEiHpteeZzvTparEZyMSJ4mIOOpHcwohhHVy4n8A0wl9z09IXHAg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('xckAySVrQ6xxoynhOAIwQTqHVCSVaU9A9jUiiKZXSzQ9IDxHybU1ntK35JtLLtxi1GNALmQWZqPmxzr0KL/10A==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/29837.Moli_re">About Molière</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/29837.Moli_re"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1219168412p3/29837.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/29837.Moli_re">Molière
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,090
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2723703894147371946">Jean-Baptiste Poquelin, also known by his stage name, Molière, was a French playwright and actor who is considered one of the greatest masters of comedy in Western literature. Among Molière's best-known dramas are <i>Le Misanthrope, (The Misanthrope), L'Ecole des femmes (The School for Wives), Tartuffe ou l'Imposteur, (Tartuffe or the Hypocrite), L'Avare ou l'École du mensonge (The Miser), Le Malade </i></span>
  <span id="freeText2723703894147371946" style="display:none">Jean-Baptiste Poquelin, also known by his stage name, Molière, was a French playwright and actor who is considered one of the greatest masters of comedy in Western literature. Among Molière's best-known dramas are <i>Le Misanthrope, (The Misanthrope), L'Ecole des femmes (The School for Wives), Tartuffe ou l'Imposteur, (Tartuffe or the Hypocrite), L'Avare ou l'École du mensonge (The Miser), Le Malade imaginaire (The Imaginary Invalid)</i>, and <i>Le Bourgeois Gentilhomme (The Bourgeois Gentleman)</i>.<br /><br />From a prosperous family and having studied at the Jesuit Clermont College (now Lycée Louis-le-Grand), Molière was well suited to begin a life in the theatre. Thirteen years as an itinerant actor helped to polish his comic abilities while he also began writing, combining <i>Commedia dell'Arte</i> elements with the more refined French comedy.<br /><br />Through the patronage of a few aristocrats including the brother of Louis XIV, Molière procured a command performance before the King at the Louvre. Performing a classic play by <i>Pierre Corneille</i> and a farce of his own, <i>Le Docteur amoureux (The Doctor in Love)</i>, Molière was granted the use of Salle du Petit-Bourbon at the Louvre, a spacious room appointed for theatrical performances. Later, Molière was granted the use of the Palais-Royal. In both locations he found success among the Parisians with plays such as <i>Les Précieuses ridicules (The Affected Ladies), L'École des maris (The School for Husbands)</i> and <i>L'École des femmes (The School for Wives)</i>. This royal favour brought a royal pension to his troupe and the title "Troupe du Roi" (The King's Troupe). Molière continued as the official author of court entertainments.<br /><br />Though he received the adulation of the court and Parisians, Molière's satires attracted criticisms from moralists and the Church. <i>Tartuffe ou l'Imposteur (Tartuffe or the Hypocrite)</i> and its attack on religious hypocrisy roundly received condemnations from the Church while <i>Don Juan</i> was banned from performance. Molière's hard work in so many theatrical capacities began to take its toll on his health and, by 1667, he was forced to take a break from the stage. In 1673, during a production of his final play, <i>Le Malade imaginaire (The Imaginary Invalid)</i>, Molière, who suffered from pulmonary tuberculosis, was seized by a coughing fit and a haemorrhage while playing the hypochondriac Argan. He finished the performance but collapsed again soon after, and died a few hours later. In his time in Paris, Molière had completely reformed French comedy.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2723703894147371946" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

This is determined by how many signed out pagehits the user has executed an how recently they have
last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
React component will render its content.
-->
